I come from a generation whos parents thought taking medication for mental health issues was extreme, taboo and whatever other adjective you can come up with to describe it as something that "normal" people just didn't do.  I was the first person in my family to seek out therapy (and I come from a family of medical professionals!).  It took me a very long time to finally decide to start taking something for my issues.  It turned out life changing for me.  Fast forward to now when I have two almost grown boys of my own and both are very in tune with mental health and wellness.  I say all of that to say this:  Medication works.  No, a pill alone will not "fix" you.  However, as part of a therapy process (whatever level meets your needs) medication can be life altering.  Anyone that may be out there wondering if it's something they should move forward with I can only say that you should discuss it with your doctor or therapist and if you determine it's right for you and know that it might just be exactly what is needed.  Just remember that it's not an instant fix or an automatic cure.  It's a tool to help you attain mental wellness.  I'm proud of you Phil!

Hi dad
I took lexapro (I think? Was branded cipralex but I'm p sure its the same drug) for several years and it was very effective. Unfortunately it was _too_ effective, and it left me in sort of a "meh" state. I had sexual side effects as well, but worst of all I could rarely find the motivation to create things. As a creative person, that was the straw that broke the camel's back. Switched to wellbutrin and things greatly improved from there.   I guess what I want to say is meds can be a lifesaver and absolutely should be used if you need them, but don't get complacent. Be mindful, remember why you're on them and what your goals are. If you don't think they're working for you properly, don't be afraid to ask for a lower or higher dose, or a different medication, or even to stop them completely. Only you know what is going on in your head, so it's your responsibility to take control of it.

I know I'm late to this video, but I wanted to say regarding Lexapro... while a pill doesn't fix everything, it's good to get your brain functioning properly. The brain adapts to situations and when it's adapted to depression, it's not easy for it to get to a point of working like a neurotypical brain would work. My depression resulted in repressed emotions and an inability to be happy; since Lexapro, that's changed a lot. Not to mention I've dealt with suicidal ideation all my life and since Day One of taking Lexapro, I nearly never think of suicide as an option.  Just don't think little of taking medication. The brain is an organ and needs to be able to function properly. Taking medication helps that. It's only one part of a process, but it's an important part, nonetheless.

Pills don‚Äôt fix things, no, but it can make things easier to manage.  It makes it easier to do what you need to do to fix things and achieve the goals you want to.  If it doesn‚Äôt work for you, then it‚Äôs okay to try something else, whether it‚Äôs another medication or ditching the meds for meditation or nutrition adjustment.  It‚Äôs about achieving the chemical balance your body/brain isn‚Äôt on its own like anyone taking an iron supplement for a deficiency or cholesterol medicine.  For me, adderall was a life changer for my adhd.  ADHD is all about underperforming dopamine receptors and it stimulates them into working properly.  Does that mean everything for me is fixed?  Fuck no.  But it‚Äôs given me the energy and ability to effectively exercise my self control that I‚Äôve been able to build habits, motivate myself, and do things I don‚Äôt want to do.  It seems underwhelming to say but when you can‚Äôt do those things and haven‚Äôt been able to, it feels monumental.  I‚Äôve never purged, but Phil I sympathize when it comes to the weight problem.  I gained 110 lbs and was nearly at 300 over 6 years after leaving for college up to starting my medication.  I‚Äôve since lost 80 of those pounds in the last 2 years because binging was one of the ways I would try to stimulate dopamine production and for a long time felt like the only thing I alone could control especially with a parent that tried so hard to control my weight and singled me out for it between me and two siblings.  So with all that said: I hope the meds work out for you and if not, I hope you find something that does.  I hope that you find the work you put in to your mental health pays off because the one person who we have to live with and please forever is ourselves. I know how hard it is not only to get help, but to recognize you need it in the first place. I was 22 the first time I realized I needed help and I got turned away from psychiatric help (probably, in part, due to me being female). I was 24 the second time I tried, because my then boyfriend and now husband was distressingly worried about me. Initially, I was diagnosed with anxiety and depression, but when I was 27, I was diagnosed ADHD and starting medication for that 180'd my life. Even though my life got so much better from that moment, solving one problem has only revealed more problems. I have some kind of weird undiagnosed sleep disorder where I can just suddenly need to immediately lie down and sleep or risk passing out. It's not narcolepsy cuz I don't just drop, but it does negatively affect my life. I haven't had time to get that checked out because they need to do a "sleep survey" and I haven't had the time to do that. And, most recently, and I guess, most distressing, I've found out I have a multiple personality disorder. None of my other diagnoses has scared me as much as this one and I'm still working with my Psychiatrist and Therapist to figure out just what the exact diagnosis for this is.   tl;dr: My reason for sharing this is that I just wanted to try and let you know that mental and emotional issues often have layers, and dealing with one, may reveal another. If this happens for you, please don't get discouraged. When the hurt and fear subsides, comfort and understanding can be found in recontextualizing and understanding your past, which is just another step toward being at peace with yourself, so you can focus on the things outside of yourself that matter to you.
While you're right, a  pill won't fix everything, Lexapro is definitely the way to go. I tried many anti-depressants and it was the only one that I saw real progress.

"I don't want you" hit me. My boy turns three in 2 months.  That said, my kiddos (and animal crossing) have been the only way that I held up.   The dietary struggles...feeling those too.  My kids pediatrician did my postpartum depression check and noted the not only was I having more trouble with this adjustment to my third ray of sunshine arriving in September, but across the board the number on moms she sees who develop PPD is markedly up, and she believes it is that covid already has us off balance.   I spent a majority of my pregnancy staying home and staying safe, and as it did protect me and mine from the virus, it left me feeling like I was losing out. I missed a lot of important family stuff. I delayed my 20 year HS reunion I had spent the last 6 months planning for "people who didn't even like" me, as my sister reminds me. (She is a therapist, sooo...wth?) But I had never attended one and you know...looked forward to a party (I am a good party planner. It was gonna be great) but also, maybe a lil bit, wanted to show my last 20 years where great despite all the nastiness I survived in those school years.  But as I type this, I am happy. My 2 month old little boy, Quill, is a light inbthe caverns of my mind. He has endured a lot in his little life. He has kidney issues that we will know, before the new year, if he is staring down surgery by 6 months of age to correct the issue. He will need to undergo a biiiiig batch of invasive testing...and it all spins in my mind as I stare down at this little angel who is beaming up at me like he hasn't a care in this world. He doesn't know the stress and fear of all of this. And he doesn't know how his sweet little smiles are helping me survive the worst depression of my life.  My family continues to splinter since we lost my Dad in 2017. For all his faults, he was the linchpin on sanity in this family and it has all gone the way of the dinosaur. So in this time of crisis, as we struggle and surely they do too, we cannot lean on one another the way we once had. I had siblings mad my mom stayed with us for a month when Quill arrived because she wasn't there for their needs. Only one truly had a legitimate complaint that I feel superceded my own request for aid, which was my eldest sister. (She endured the loss of her left foot to gangrene as a complication to her spina bifida, which she has fought for 3 decades, and she wasn't even supposed to live past 13, so she is a miracle truly. And she has named her leg Stumpy and is somehow surviving like a champ. I wish I had that strength.)  All said, I wanted to say "thank you" for sharing your life with us, and opening up on your own struggles. It is connection that makes us truly human, and we've been disconnected for so very long. It reminds me of that moment in Into the Spiderverse where Peter and Miles met and said "you're like me".   Subbed to this channel, as well as your main channel and many subchannels, and look forward to future chances to make a human connection, even for a few minutes.   I'm rooting for you.

Meds don't fix everything. But they sure as hell do take the edge off the stress of existence enough for you to fix the problems around you. People talk a lot about the stigma around mental illness. There is a separate stigma around medication. It is a tool, not a magic bullet. Just like therapy is a tool. It is not the easy way out. You still have to do the work. Therapy is a shovel. Therapy and meds is like a snowblower. More effective but it still isn't going to dig you out unless you push it yourself.
I've been in therapy for four years and my life has never been better. Just popping the stigma bubble.

This year has been a weird year, but what I've learned more than anything is, the value of a Hug is undeniable.  - Last December I left a job of 14 years to go all in on a business I had started at the beginning of 2019. Had I known this year was going to happen I'd probably have stayed, but I keep telling myself if I can get through this year, then hopefully that means the business can weather anything thrown at it. - That last part is hard to tell yourself though, when you see the savings decreasing every month to keep you afloat. - Of course, one benefit to being in lockdown, no spending money on socialising. So the savings are going further. Makes you realise how much money you don't need to spend on life :)  - I live alone, and I live in a town where I don't know anyone, so there isn't an easy go for a walk with a local friend. This didn't really matter, or register, when work and social life were in a city centre, but lockdown rules made it all to aware how far I live from everyone I know.  - Pretty much everyone I know has been sticking to the UK tier 3 rules (which for the Greater Manchester area has been pretty much the norm all year, as they are near identical to National Lockdown rules), means very rarely seen anyone physically. At one point I saw someone's Dad randomly in the street and ended up talking to them for so long I think they probably wanted to run away lol, but good guy who gave me some time to chat for 10 minutes.  - Since February, I finally saw 'some' family for the first time in November for an hour, but most family live on the other side of the Country so no idea when we'll see each other, seems weird to be talking about meeting up earliest probably Easter.  - I've seen 3 friends this year, we went walks outside during the Summer a handful of times. But as Autumn came along and then Winter, none of us really want to be out in the rain.  - I got really ill at one point resulting in sepsis, because I hadn't been being active enough (easy to do when work is at the same desk you do any downtime activity as well), ended up in AE with a bunch of family worried because lockdown meant they couldn't come to the hospital. Fortunately, wasn't in there long, they actually wanted me out asap to minimise the chance of me being near any covid patients.  - I used to talk to my Dad pretty regularly on the phone as he lived on the South coast, but he passed away in Nov 2019. So, the year has had the addition of being weird of trying to not really come to terms with but adjusting to him not being there anymore. While I've been ok with that, it has been hard to help family who aren't coping with his loss as well, not being able to travel across the country on a whim to comfort them is tough.  - It makes you realise how much of a difference it is actually seeing people face to face is, you can do video chat etc, but it isn't the same. While I value my own space, I like my own space on my own terms :p  - As to eating disorders and depression, I totally get that. I think trying to kick food right now would be a step too far for me, plus it's been with me for probably 25-30 years. The weight is definitely up this year, and the depression is a familiar bed fellow. I'm just glad I managed to kick booze into touch 3 years ago, as I doubt I'd still be typing if I'd gone into this year needing the sauce it would have been way to easy to just drink myself away with no one around to stop me.

Wow, 256lbs, you must be hella tall because I would have never ever guessed. Turns out I was a 'severe chronic depressant' for like 12 years (only 2 years of symptoms needed to qualify.) I got back from the war and just gradually felt my self change and being in my mid 20's I figured it was a normal thing. My thoughts got darker at an exponential rate and within a year of coming home, I regularly though of suicide. Many times I would hold a knife to my throat and think 'one strong swift stroke, and all of it would be over' then I would think of the aftermath and I didn't want people to have to clean up after me, or my family to go through that. So I settled on killing myself slowly with 'self medication' and figured I'd die at around 50-60 of heart disease and could then my next of kin could claim my life insurance. I spent about a decade and a half just waiting to die. That's how thought out I was at that point. I've developed chronic 'diseases' because of this along with advanced stages of tooth decay and a number of other things that you get with just waiting for death like I have to force myself to brush my teeth and shower regularly. Things that I think most people do as a habit, I have to re-learn as a habit. Because for so long I just didn't care because I just wanted to die. I would have never thought someone as successful as you could have a form of mental illness, but look at Robin Williams. For a long long time, I also could not understand how someone could be genuinely happy, like actually hole heartedly happy. I thought everyone thought about killing themselves at least a few times a week. I thought that was normal, until I got help. During one of my hangovers from self-medicating I was talking to someone in a military gaming group I'm part of (MilitaryGamers.com) and he/she pointed me towards the right direction and I realized that the only way I wasn't going to end up as a statistic or seeing my family grieve at my grave was to make the call. I did, I got help from the counselor along with the VA hospital. I took medication for at least 6 months, maybe a year. At least now I not only don't want to end my life, I look forward to the future. I'm still working on forming basic habits that I think most people already have of self care, but at least I'm headed in the right direction. You're definitely not alone Phil. Too many people out there are suffering from mental illness and the more we are open to talk about it, maybe more people will get help and know there is light at the end of the tunnel. I know I could never imagine a life with happiness for many years, but it can happen.
Phillip Neistat

As I've learned in the last year, there's no shame in admitting that you need more help than therapy alone can give.  I live with PTSD from some pretty severe childhood traumas, and for years, I fought the idea of taking medication for my disability/ chronic illness. It was only in the last year, and with the love and support of my husband and close friends that I came to terms with my need for chemical assistance. I started taking a small dose of sertraline almost half a year ago in conjunction with ongoing therapy and self care, and I cannot stress enough how much it has helped. I went from constant nightmares, lethargy, panic attacks, self harming idealization, and depression, to finally being able to sleep through the night and go out in public on my own again.   It's a journey, and sometimes the steps are scary and difficult, but there's always a beautiful view at the top of the mountain. Hang in there, Phil.

I would rather not be taking medication for my mental issues, however it‚Äôs a normal medical issue and sometimes you simply need the medication.  I‚Äôve been working with my MD for years now trying to find what works best for me changing the dose on one and adding another.  It‚Äôs amazing the difference that it makes.  And it‚Äôs not like I turn into a robot or zombie when taking them, and if you‚Äôre having side effects that interfere with your quality of life it‚Äôs time to talk to your doctor about trying something else.    It‚Äôs a real medical issue and some people can work through it with seeing a therapist and making changes in their life, and others need something more.  It‚Äôs no different than having other issues like high BP, or diabetes, some people can keep things in check by changing their lifestyle but others may need to take a medication.  The only difference is that mental disorders were taboo for so long and everything was so hush hush (which is why I can‚Äôt get that portion of my medical history even though I know certain people had serious issue). I have major depressive disorder, recurrent, along with anxiety and panic attacks, big deal, I take medication which helps, meet with my therapist regularly, and my Psychiatrist every three months to make sure my meds are working.  The only real issue is that not everyone understands that I‚Äôm not just depressed or anxious, and all that I can do is explain what it is that I go through. I've a Desert Storm vet which primarily means (at least for me) that mental health is always a concern and confronting my various problems is priority. But it wasn't until 2020 that depression became a serious issue. I am the kind of person who likes to go out and meet people, I socialized regularly because it is good for my mental balance. The major down side is that  I also have adult-onset asthma which makes Covid-19 exceptionally dangerous for me and I am restricted to the house because the options are literally Live or Die.  So I contacted the VA and they set me up with a psychologist and we worked through it and now I'm on Sertraline to help with my depression. Medication doesn't fix anything, because you can't fix your mind; what medication does is allow you to not be dragged down by what ails you. It allows you to cope in a healthy manner. Good on you for getting the help you need and after this whole insanity is over, maybe we can put the meds away and get back to life as it should be. Best of luck to you.

Can I just idk.... I don't know the words I'm looking for ... just thank you. For sharing this, for showing people "normal" looking people can struggle with an eating disorder, for showing people "good" parents can struggle with an eating disorder, for publicly talking about it, for showing people men can struggle with it also... for making me realize I should maybe start thinking about seeing someone.   I started not eating when I was 12 not as a way to loose weight but as a way to have some sort of control/structure/deal with physical abuse and neglect. I got help at 17 and haven't had any problems until this year. I'm 28 5'11" and my weight today is 128lbs... This year has just been unimaginably difficult from being high risk because of autoimmune disorders and medications to not leaving the house with an autistic/ADHD/ODD 9 year old and 3 year old sense March to home schooling to my husband having to quarantine every few weeks and loosing income and praying we don't get evicted like 45% of the people in our complex because most of his coworkers don't believe the virus is real ...  I don't actively try not to eat, I don't plan it out, I don't obsess over it. It usually isn't until I'm so hungry that it hurts or I have a hard time doing anything that I realize I haven't eaten in 2 days or I'll open a snack like chips/sit down with a plate of food fully intending on eating all of it and instead I'll take a few bites and then start offering it to my kids or "save it for later" on the counter until my husband throws it away. Around June my husband started noticing and going out of his way to ask why I wasn't finishing my plate and even started making it a point to sit down and eat next to me (we have a tiny apartment with no table so my 3 year old eats at the coffee table, 9 year old at the computer desk and then I usually eat in the room on the bed and my husband eats with the kids on the couch) and eat as slowly as I am or flat out just bugging me until I finish. We've talked about my past with anorexia and I can tell he's getting really worried but I don't know how to muster up the courage to do anything about it. I'm afraid of judgement, I'm afraid for my kids, I'm afraid of I guess loosing that control...

Anyone whos feeling like a waste of space during this quarantine, take my advice: Pick up a creative hobby, something to put hours into, with a visible (non-cash) payout at the end. I'm generally in a dark place, but this year I started REALLY trying to draw, and draw consistently and I feel a little better about myself every time I notice a genuine improvement in my art.   Theres options, even for people without the "creative" spark. Get into model kit building, get lego sets, learn to draw. It doesnt really matter what type of "creative" outlet, but something "hands on" with a visible payout (finished work/model/art/etc)  If you need a suggestion, or lack funds (but have SOME kind of pc) I tend to suggest 3D modelling in Blender. You can learn to create/model real world objects, or other peoples works, so you dont need that "creative" imagination as much, plus Blender is 100% totally and completely free. Even a potatoe can do SOMETHING with blender (rending may take a while for low end systems)   Heck, even start doodling on your mobile device, theres a bunch of free apps there (at least for android) and even if its your finger tip doodles, its something to start!

Although I still took waaaaay too long to get help for my anxiety and depression I feel like I was lucky to be a woman and feel like I had a lot less stigma to deal with, I cannot imagine how hard it can be for men to talk about this stuff and be so vulnerable. And it did take me years to realise there was nothing wrong with needing fluoxetine or CBT to stay on top of my life, now that I'm there I am the biggest advocate for trying medication (it might work, it might not but I believe everyone should give it a chance if their brain chemistry is fundamentally screwed up like mine is) and seeing a therapist before things have a chance to snowball, to the point I think I may sometimes piss off my friends raving about how important it is to get help and try every avenue to find what works for you because something will.  I'm so sorry to hear you're struggling with an ED, I suffered from anorexia nervosa for years and admittedly it is still a struggle a decade after becoming "healthy" so I'll probably consider myself to be in recovery for the rest of my life. I relate to a lot of this, Phil, and I think it's very cool that you're so open and willing to share and willing to be vulnerable, even though it's difficult. I'm trying to make a lot of the same changes as you, I'm trying to get in better shape, I'm trying to get a handle on my mental health, I'm trying to get a better handle on my ... I won't say disordered eating, but it's definitely borderline. It's rough going. I have a friend I talk to literally every day who is helping to coach me through parts of it because a lot of the time I just need someone else to be accountable to because I don't care enough about myself to help myself. But it's a journey, and I like that we are apparently working through it together, it makes me feel less alone.   To anyone concerned who might want elaboration on my "borderline disordered eating", I have something of a cycle I'm in where I just keep forgetting to eat until I get way too skinny and then I become disgusted with my appearance and throw myself into working out and counting calories to get myself back to a healthy weight and look... until I start to slack off and then I start to forget again. And the not eating enough thing isn't so much a self image thing that I do on purpose as much as it's a few different factors all coming to a head. I have a chronic migraine/facial pain disorder, and I'm the sort of person who, if I'm in pain, I don't want to eat. And since I'm essentially always in pain, I don't eat. That, plus I'm on ADHD medication which also has an appetite suppressing side effect, but otherwise works really well for me, so I'm reluctant to switch to something else. There's also a tiny bit of self image in there too, I won't lie to you. When I get a good handle on my eating  but I'm not exercising enough and I gain more fat, I do get that voice in my head saying that maaaaybe I should start forgetting to eat again until my double chin goes away. But I try not to let that guide my decisions.

Hey!  I take Lexapro too!  For all of my living memory, I've had severe physical symptoms from anxiety.  Increased heart rate, increased fear/paranoia, tense muscles (particularly in my abdomen).  That little pill is a workhorse.  It's been almost 3 years now that I've been on it, with no side effects (age 27 through 30).  Like you said in the video, it doesn't cure anything, it's not a silver bullet.  But for me, it relieves a lot of those physical symptoms, which has been lifechanging.  I hope it works out for you too Phil!
Lexapro really helped me, but it triggered "celebratory eating" in me (feeling better, wanting to celebrate feeling better with favorite foods, which make me feel better, so I celebrate...), so I gained 35 pounds in 3 months üòÇ. Taking Wellbutrin now, which helps my depression and ADHD but also doesn't give me that same feeling of "everything is so chill let's eat an entire cake!"
